<div id="review_1624920307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1624920307" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1624920307?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=comedy-sorta">comedy-sorta</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1624920307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9171974337279890197"><b>Brilliant Brit</b><br/><br/>I enjoyed this wonderful book immensely, and would recommend it enthusiastically to all my British family and friends—except that all my British friends have already read it! My only hesitation in an American context is that people who have not grown up in postwar Britain as Kate Atkinson (and I) did might not get her dense texture of forgotten brand-names and vanished social customs. In this, she is pitch-perfect, recalling not only the lost era of her own childhood but also the E</span>
  <span id="freeText9171974337279890197" style="display:none"><b>Brilliant Brit</b><br /><br />I enjoyed this wonderful book immensely, and would recommend it enthusiastically to all my British family and friends—except that all my British friends have already read it! My only hesitation in an American context is that people who have not grown up in postwar Britain as Kate Atkinson (and I) did might not get her dense texture of forgotten brand-names and vanished social customs. In this, she is pitch-perfect, recalling not only the lost era of her own childhood but also the England of her grandparents and parents. I can see why there is a Reader's Guide on the market, for Atkinson's museum contains an almost archaeological treasure that, though sometimes needing annotation, is always authentic. <br /><br />But her factual authenticity is nothing compared to her authenticity as a person. For the story of the growth of her narrator-heroine Ruby Lennox from conception into young womanhood is that of a life not so much described as lived. I assume that the book is largely autobiographical, but it does not read as that either, since Ruby (who always speaks in a voice older than her years, and can be marvelously funny) is so much alive that she leaps off the page as an independent creation, clearly informed by the author's love-hate relationship with the other members of her extended family, but by no means following her footsteps. It is almost a pity at the end when she comes of age, and the provocative double focus of adult/child merges into one. <br /><br />The various chapters of Ruby's story, told chronologically, are interspersed with long "footnotes" investigating different episodes in the lives of her mother, grandmother, and great-grandmother (this is a feminist book without making a fetish of it), their siblings, and the various men who affect their lives. These stretch back into the late 19th century, spanning two world wars, and show (as other readers have pointed out) how much the lives of women have changed in the past century. I found that many of the most moving parts of the book, some of which brought tears to my eyes, were contained in these sections. But they are told out of chronological order and feature a large cast of characters; I wish I had thought to jot down a timeline and family tree as I was reading. <br /><br />Various reviews quoted in the paperback edition compare Atkinson to Dickens. Although her voice is quite different, there are certain similarities: both authors enjoy mystery and belated revelations, both have a fondness for highly colored characters, both share a comic vision, and both tend towards the melodramatic. There is more than the average amount of tragedy in this book, and few of their lives follow predictable paths. But Atkinson's characters do not lose reality because of this but seem more vigorously alive. Given Atkinson's infectious voice and grasp on life, the overall effect of this book is wonderfully exhilarating. <br /><br />+ + + + + +<br /><br />For another magnificently authentic account of life in Britain in the years around WW2, I recommend Andrea Levy's recent <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44001.Small_Island" title="Small Island by Andrea Levy" rel="nofollow noopener">Small Island</a>.</i> And for a book that attempts a somewhat similar family saga to Atkinson's, but in an American context, I suggest Carol Shields' modern classic <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77554.The_Stone_Diaries" title="The Stone Diaries by Carol Shields" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stone Diaries</a>.</i> Both these books are among the best I have read in 2006; <i>Behind The Scenes</i> will make a third.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9171974337279890197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2698914323" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2698914323" />
    <a title="Issicratea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23268828-issicratea"><img alt="Issicratea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378040602p2/23268828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2698914323?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Issicratea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Issicratea" href="/user/show/23268828-issicratea">Issicratea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23268828-issicratea?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23268828-issicratea?shelf=1990-2010">1990-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2698914323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer721108740230878582">This is an engaging, witty, slips-down-easy novel, aiming for greater depth and bite as it meanders towards its ending. In some ways, it reminded me of Anna Burns’s recent <i>Milkman</i> (the snarky, under-age narrator; the eccentric family; the surface cynicism muting into eventual edgy warmth)—although I felt <i>Milkman</i> had a lot more to say.<br/><br/><i>Behind the Scenes</i> works on a number of chronological planes. The lead narrative is a coming-of-age piece, centered on the story of a girl born in York in 1952, Ruby</span>
  <span id="freeText721108740230878582" style="display:none">This is an engaging, witty, slips-down-easy novel, aiming for greater depth and bite as it meanders towards its ending. In some ways, it reminded me of Anna Burns’s recent <i>Milkman</i> (the snarky, under-age narrator; the eccentric family; the surface cynicism muting into eventual edgy warmth)—although I felt <i>Milkman</i> had a lot more to say.<br /><br /><i>Behind the Scenes</i> works on a number of chronological planes. The lead narrative is a coming-of-age piece, centered on the story of a girl born in York in 1952, Ruby Lennox. This is all plainly autobiographical, and none the worse for that. (Atkinson herself was born in York in 1951.) <br /><br />Stacked up behind Ruby’s story, in a series of <i>faux</i> footnotes, are the stories of her mother, and her grandmother and great-grandmother—and of any number of uncles and great-uncles, and cousins. Taken together, these sub-stories combine with the main story to make up some kind of collective domestic biography of the whole twentieth century in the UK, incorporating both world wars and the social changes that accompanied them. There is a certain—discreet—focus on women, and on their lack of choices in life, but this is far from exclusive. The novel is also good on the dynamic of “woman hands on misery to woman.” <br /><br />I don’t want to be too ponderous in reviewing <i>Behind the Scenes</i>, because it's not ponderous itself. Some of it is very funny, especially the set-piece episodes. I liked the extended accounts of a disastrous family holiday in Scotland in the 70s, and of an equally disastrous family wedding soon afterwards. <br /><br />This is a novel rather free in its borrowings (the opening scene is imitated from <i>Tristram Shandy</i>, and there is a flagrant borrowing from Ariosto later on, in the form of the “lost property locker” philosophy of life). Yet it is equally generous to those who come after—I can’t believe, for example, that Ian McEwan was not influenced in his <i>Nutmeg</i> by Atkinson’s treatment of the knowing foetal voice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="721108740230878582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Siria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115473-siria"><img alt="Siria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332731316p2/115473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13152288?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siria" href="/user/show/115473-siria">Siria</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13152288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4555549755806118183">This is a first novel, and it does show in a couple of places--the early chapters struggle to maintain the plausibility of such an adult authorial voice being refracted through the experience and understanding of a child, and there's at least one plot twist towards the end of the novel which I thought it could well have done without. Despite that, I really loved this book: the humour of it is just right for me, balanced right on the edge of tragedy. The prose achieves moments of real loveliness,</span>
  <span id="freeText4555549755806118183" style="display:none">This is a first novel, and it does show in a couple of places--the early chapters struggle to maintain the plausibility of such an adult authorial voice being refracted through the experience and understanding of a child, and there's at least one plot twist towards the end of the novel which I thought it could well have done without. Despite that, I really loved this book: the humour of it is just right for me, balanced right on the edge of tragedy. The prose achieves moments of real loveliness, without ever seeming to strive for the correct turn of phrase, and her observances of her characters are both painful and real. I'll definitely be keeping an eye out for her other works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4555549755806118183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1358224669" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1358224669" />
    <a title="Will Ansbacher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5072847-will-ansbacher"><img alt="Will Ansbacher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485713675p2/5072847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1358224669?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Ansbacher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Ansbacher" href="/user/show/5072847-will-ansbacher">Will Ansbacher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5072847-will-ansbacher?shelf=dysfunction">dysfunction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1358224669" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14648880072801666058">This was her first book? Wow, Ian McEwan should have done so well! <br/><br/>BtS@tM is a tale about Ruby, born in 1952, and her family, in particular her dysfunctional relationship with her mother Bunty; and brilliantly told it is too, beginning with Ruby’s own conception: “I exist!” <br/>Atkinson has a sharp and sardonic wit when it comes to family dysfunction and the novel opens with an air thick with disappointment, as Ruby relates the dire trap that Bunty’s loveless marriage has become: because Atkinson pe</span>
  <span id="freeText14648880072801666058" style="display:none">This was her first book? Wow, Ian McEwan should have done so well! <br /><br />BtS@tM is a tale about Ruby, born in 1952, and her family, in particular her dysfunctional relationship with her mother Bunty; and brilliantly told it is too, beginning with Ruby’s own conception: “I exist!” <br />Atkinson has a sharp and sardonic wit when it comes to family dysfunction and the novel opens with an air thick with disappointment, as Ruby relates the dire trap that Bunty’s loveless marriage has become: because Atkinson performs the clever trick of having Ruby comment on her own life as she experienced it at the time, (even before her birth), but at the same time with an adult’s knowingness, looking back on those same events. And the amazing thing is, it doesn’t feel like an artifice at all. <br /><br />The story encompasses Ruby and her immediate family – older sisters and parents - and a crowd of uncles, aunts and cousins, plus the parallel lives of the previous couple of generations through “footnotes” at the end of each chapter - which are really separate chapters covering her parents’ and grandparents’ generations. Yes, there are a <i>lot</i> of characters ... Bunty is the middle one of five siblings, <i>her</i> mother was the youngest of seven, but they quickly take on their own idiosyncrasies and it isn’t that hard to keep them apart.<br /><br />Although BtS@tM continues to the present (1992, when the book was written), it essentially ends with the uncovering, at the age of 16, of the secret of Ruby’s deep unhappiness ...<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the suppressed memory of her twin sister Pearl, drowned when she was 4, and for which she had been unjustly blamed by Gillian, the middle sister. Ruby’s suppressed memory of the horrific event had been amplified by her family’s resolve never to mention Pearl to her again, though she appears ghost-like throughout the book, as a shadow or loss that Ruby can sense but can’t see, and in several allusions to P-— and to precious jewels (plural). Long before that revelation, the family had fallen apart – Gillian was killed in a car accident and Patricia, the oldest, had left home as soon as she could. So effectively, Ruby was the only survivor. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Some people saw all this immediately, but I didn’t until I re-read the whole thing. <br /><br />In many ways, I thought this was a much more successful book than <i>Life After Life</i>, but it’s not as ambitious with the convoluted “timey-wimey” stuff either, although you can definitely see the germ of that inventiveness here. The footnote chapters weave back and forward, although Ruby’s main narrative is strictly one way.<br /><br />What stops BtS@tM being truly five stars for me is the rush with which too many lives are dispatched – in particular, Ruby's most recent 25 years, the existence of her children, her reunion with Patricia and Bunty’s death, take up barely 30 pages; Bunty’s aunt Lillian’s flight to Canada and <i>her</i> life, her children and grandchildren, are similarly dealt with in just a couple of pages. In the cascade of events, there’s barely time to mention more than the fact that X had two children, Y and Z. And while I enjoyed the “footnote” chapters that were Ruby’s ancestors’ stories, they did not have the vibrancy of Ruby’s first-person account.<br /><br />But some of the vignettes – behind the scenes, so to speak - are priceless: an unforgettable holiday in Whitby when the sisters were young, cared for by a “friend” of her father’s who actually did care for them far more than her father or mother did; a holiday from hell in Scotland with a family who lived next door; her uncle’s wedding which had been scheduled, most unfortunately, on the day of the World Cup final. Atkinson is simply brilliant at family dynamics – and the more dysfunctional, the better.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14648880072801666058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2073699891" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="DebsD" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59633249-debsd"><img alt="DebsD" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485019761p2/59633249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2073699891?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DebsD" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DebsD" href="/user/show/59633249-debsd">DebsD</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/25800169-behind-the-scenes-at-the-museum">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=rating-4-stars-and-a-bit">rating-4-stars-and-a-bit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=rating-4-stars">rating-4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=love">love</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=family-home">family-home</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=period">period</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2073699891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14213658989182999356">This took me a bit longer to get hooked by than the previous Atkinson books I've read, but it was so worth it. It's a beautifully-constructed, multigenerational family saga (a type of book I don't usually like, but this one works). The writing is gorgeous, with wonderfully perceptive illustrations of family relationships and vivid, flawed, sometimes flailing, but often (eventually) endearing characters. Atkinson's humour brings this tragicomedy away from being bleak and into something that feels</span>
  <span id="freeText14213658989182999356" style="display:none">This took me a bit longer to get hooked by than the previous Atkinson books I've read, but it was so worth it. It's a beautifully-constructed, multigenerational family saga (a type of book I don't usually like, but this one works). The writing is gorgeous, with wonderfully perceptive illustrations of family relationships and vivid, flawed, sometimes flailing, but often (eventually) endearing characters. Atkinson's humour brings this tragicomedy away from being bleak and into something that feels - well, that feels very much like real life! I loved how the layers were gradually peeled away, with the reader getting to know many of Ruby's ancestors one-by-one and revealing the family secrets. A thoroughly enjoyable read - all the more impressive given that it's Atkinson's debut novel. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14213658989182999356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_95103865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95103865" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3191832-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606294444p2/3191832.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95103865?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/3191832-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3191832-lisa?shelf=british">british</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95103865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer852339546610478646">This book was beyond tedious. An excellent example of why our English teachers were always badgering us over run-on sentences. I never saw so many commas in my life. The narrative was endless. Endless details of the mundane and uninteresting. And it was written in a way that told me the author was reaching to write in a unique style, and that always bugs me. I will definitely be avoiding all other work by this author. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_811764485" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/811764485" />
    <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus"><img alt="Ms.pegasus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335705124p2/5015851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/811764485?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms.pegasus" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus">Ms.pegasus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11255011-behind-the-scenes-at-the-museum">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who loves well-written fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer811764485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8221887936086182866">The year is 1951 and Atkinson gives us Ruby, our unconventional, imaginative narrator who greets us with the exuberant opening revelation: <i>“I exist!”</i> At this moment, our narrator has just been conceived, deep within her mother Bunty's womb. The environs provide sustenance but little else. Instead, Ruby treats us to a ring-side seat to the endless rounds of discontent played out between Bunty and her husband George. An unassuming observer, Ruby gives us the benefit of her droll commentary, able t</span>
  <span id="freeText8221887936086182866" style="display:none">The year is 1951 and Atkinson gives us Ruby, our unconventional, imaginative narrator who greets us with the exuberant opening revelation: <i>“I exist!”</i> At this moment, our narrator has just been conceived, deep within her mother Bunty's womb. The environs provide sustenance but little else. Instead, Ruby treats us to a ring-side seat to the endless rounds of discontent played out between Bunty and her husband George. An unassuming observer, Ruby gives us the benefit of her droll commentary, able to relate even the unimaginative hostility of her mother's dreams. She will soon (well, in 9 months) be joining two sisters: 5 year old brooding Patricia, and nearly 3 year old Gillian, the golden girl, the up to now baby of the family and center of all attention. What a surprise Gillian is in for, Ruby muses laconically.<br /><br />The setting is York, England – established as a fortress by the Romans, garrisoned by the famed lost legion – the Ninth, reinventing itself under the Anglo-Saxons, the Vikings, the Normans, and even into modern times after heavy bombardment during World War II. It's a city filled with ancient buildings and historic events. <br /><br />The meaning of history – not the capital H kind, but the small personal accretions over time – is the core of this novel. We follow Ruby's life through a series of comedically awful family events including the extended family gathering to watch the coronation of Elizabeth II, a poorly thought-out disaster of a vacation to Scotland (a Hibernian farmhouse in a countryside village that sounds like 'Och-na-cock-a-leekie') and the wedding of Uncle Ted on a day which happens to coincide with the World Cup final (between England and Germany, no less). <br /><br />In addition to their narrative function, these events introduce us to three preceding generations of the matrilineal line. First, there are Bunty's siblings, Clifford, Babs, Betty, and Ted. Their mother is Nell, the youngest of an even larger clutch of siblings: Ada (died in childhood), William (Ada's twin, died in infancy), Lawrence, Tom, Albert, and Lillian. We are introduced to their mother, Alice Barker, through a photograph taken in June of 1888. Their stories are slipped in cleverly through the device of “footnotes” – brief interludes that tell the individual stories of other marital disappointments, foolish choices, lucky happenstance, pointless stoicism, poignant disillusion, and untimely deaths. Nell was part of the “lost generation,” and Atkinson makes us feel that sense of loss through the faded, barely remembered stories of these people. Such brief and intense lives, so easily forgotten. An aged Nell tries to remember a boy from her youth – a boy she once hoped to marry: <i>“She stood in front of the mirror to brush her hair and put a dab of powder on her nose and tried to remember the smell of Jack Keech's skin and the feel of his hair, but it was so long ago now that she couldn't even remember what he looked like. It began to rain, a light summer shower, and the smell of the rain on the new June grass made Nell feel suddenly wretched.”</i> Ruby considers the June 1888 photograph of Alice and thinks: “Nell – who on this hot day is unborn and has all her life ahead of her – will one day be my grandmother and have all her life behind her without ever knowing how that happened (another woman lost in time).” The real sense of loss is as much the sense of forgotten memories as of death.<br /><br />In these vignettes, even Bunty can be looked at as a sympathetic character. One memory finds her weeping on a railway platform. She has missed the train to a Sunday school outing to Scarborough because her forgetful mother Nell could not be troubled to allow enough time for the children to get to the station. The slowest, Bunty misses the train that her siblings run to and successfully catch. <br /><br />The devastation of World War I is conveyed in a series of reverberating events. It claims the life of ever optimistic and cherubic Albert, and leaves even the survivors with a sense of certain impending death. The confrontation with horror leaves an unbreachable gulf between those who went to war and those who didn't. Horrible things happen to individual characters but perhaps the most telling is that the men on leave never mention anything about the war to their families. <br /><br />Atkinson holds the reader's attention by planting several mysteries. In the opening chapters she alludes to Gillian's untimely end, piquing our curiosity. We wonder how Ruby will fare in the midst of her dysfunctional family. In addition there's a strange lacuna in her childhood story, and 4-year old Ruby relates living for a time with Aunt Babs, haunted by unnamed fears and nights of sleepwalking. Throughout the story, some of the characters turn their backs on the options at hand and we wonder about their fates. Finally, Atkinson comes to disclose certain secrets about the life of Alice Barker which were omitted from the unreliable narratives of her children. <br /><br />Atkinson also preserves a sense of continuity. Nell's stepmother, Rachel, keeps a rabbit's foot – a good luck token, and, in an unwonted impulse of generosity, gives it to one of Albert's friends when they depart for the War. The rabbit's foot ends up among Bunty's belongings. Alice's silver locket, given to her by her grandfather, is tucked under Lillian's pillow, and she later gives it to Nell who was too young to ever know their mother. There are other objects that turn up through the generations, and references to a gradually diminishing set of chinaware decorated with forget-me-nots. An old clock that once belonged to Alice's mother Sophie is another object that passes from generation to generation. Finally, there are out of body experiences, perhaps a genetic predisposition encouraged by the ancient history of York itself, or perhaps a gift to those in the family with a particular sensibility. Those experiences provide a satisfying contemplation of the connection between life and death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8221887936086182866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_329695747" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329695747" />
    <a title="Dolly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1019021-dolly"><img alt="Dolly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1223325529p2/1019021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/329695747?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolly" href="/user/show/1019021-dolly">Dolly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781417646609" href="/book/show/6928150-behind-the-scenes-at-the-museum">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of historical fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=cooking-recipes">cooking-recipes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=australia">australia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=easter">easter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=south-central-america-mexico">south-central-america-mexico</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=fitness-sports">fitness-sports</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=aviation">aviation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1019021-dolly?shelf=jewish-israel">jewish-israel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer329695747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9286253528066378373">I found this book while passing through England and I thought it would be a good read. I am so glad I discovered it. The story weaves its way backward and forward through time, tracing the path of several people's lives - their loves, their deaths, their experiences. I love that it primarily tracks the women of one family and it's fascinating to see the way in which their lives intertwine. <br/><br/>It takes place primarily in England in the early 1950s and 1960s, but the story reaches from the late 1890s</span>
  <span id="freeText9286253528066378373" style="display:none">I found this book while passing through England and I thought it would be a good read. I am so glad I discovered it. The story weaves its way backward and forward through time, tracing the path of several people's lives - their loves, their deaths, their experiences. I love that it primarily tracks the women of one family and it's fascinating to see the way in which their lives intertwine. <br /><br />It takes place primarily in England in the early 1950s and 1960s, but the story reaches from the late 1890s to the early 1990s. I thought it was quite appropriate that one of the highlights of the book is Queen Elizabeth's coronation. Everywhere I look (here in England), I see souvenirs for the Queen's Diamond Jubilee (as well as for the upcoming 2012 summer Olympics) and I really enjoyed reading about the experiences of those who witnessed her become Queen, even if it's within the context of a fictional tale. <br /><br />I liked the use of English idioms, many of which were new to me. It really made me enjoy my stay in England that much more, knowing that I was learning a bit more about England and its culture and people. <br /><br />I also liked the way in which the narrative reads through Ruby's eyes and her thoughts. It initially shows her innate wisdom as a just-formed fetus/ancient soul while in utero. The story then progresses to her early childhood and the narrative demonstrates the naive, wide-eyed wonder and rambling thoughts of a young child who lives very much in the present. We see her fears and her imagination, especially regarding the dark and the ghosts who are haunting her house. As Ruby ages, she becomes more aware, but remains very ingenuous, showing her innocence in her observations. Later, as Ruby becomes an adult and later approaches midlife, we witness her discovery of some of the more unsavory truths in life, but we also see her acceptance of the people she loves. <br /><br />Overall, I thought this was a very intriguing, though somewhat melancholy story. I loved being a voyeur into the lives of the four generations of women, watching as they lived and died, seeing the linkages, even if they did not. So many times I just wanted one of them to see or know something that they were oblivious to, something that the author made so obvious to the reader. But that was really part of the charm of the story and what made it seem all so real. I really enjoyed reading this book and I will look for other books by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10015.Kate_Atkinson" title="Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Kate Atkinson</a>. <br /><br />interesting quotes:<br /><i>"...after Albert was dead, Jack realized that Albert collected good days the way other people collected coins, or sets of postcards."</i> (p. 68)<br /><br /><i>"Notre mère," Patricia remarks sweetly, "est une vache, n'est-ce pas?"</i> (p. 214)<br /><br /><i>"Bunty must still expect Patricia home because she has left her room untouched, and as Patricia was not the tidiest of girls and her room was always littered with dirty clothing and food crumbs, it has by now taken on a Miss Haversham-air of decay and will probably soon revert to primordial slime."</i> (p. 249)<br /><br /><i>"I've lived too long anyhow and when I'm dead I'll be with my children and that's the only place a mother really wants to be."</i> (p. 273)<br /><br /><i>"I have been to the world's end and back and now I know what I would put in my bottom drawer. I would put my sisters."</i> (p. 295)<br /><br /><i>"The past is what you leave behind in life, Ruby," she says with the smile of a reincarnated lama. "Nonsense, Patricia," I tell her as I climb on board my train. "The past's what you take with you."</i> (p. 331)<br /><br />new words: tricoteuse, hypomanic, parkin, conchies, furbelows, avuncular, gavottes, thyrotoxic, squiffy, telluric, hermeneutic, parlous, tumbril, iconoclast, echolalia, anchorite, glossolalia, haywain, sellotape, bannocks, slattern, augury, pibroch, coracle, distaff, crimpolene, soroptimist, gurning, stultifying, plimsolls, cachous, quatrefoil, quattrocento, epithalamion, uxorial, fritillaries, buskers </span>
  <a data-text-id="9286253528066378373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText9917784495808778383" style="display:none">who [...] will one day [...] have all her life behind her without ever knowing how that happened (another woman lost in time).<br />*<br />This pessimistic outlook was a source of considerable comfort to her - after all, unhappiness could be relied upon in a way that happiness never could.<br />*<br />Patricia embraces me on the station platform.   function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Q8aCMH50Yi6psiKXzxku6xZczjVJyG/mpwuWqXNAWM1oAOfEderjLokj1lWA/1ESPKElVhJjLWacNDqRQEzMsQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QdLyrEx8BTIen/hXLHXNpMFoQdJvZtCIORoLuFLUXuhqFJdYR+KEMj4ODJVjk7Jd65WqsTTNkggCJaeAYdjKlA==')})
  }

He tries to share his love and knowledge of things in nature with her, but she does not seem interested. Teddy is upset by the fact that Izzie claims to have eaten a skylark. Teddy hates the books and the fact that they are dedicated to him.When World War II begins, Teddy signs up. As her remains are bundled into oblivion, Thomas Cromwell breakfasts wi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3676299009416307430\" style=\"display:none\">“If you cannot speak truth at a beheading, when can you speak it?”\n\nEngland, May 1536. Anne Boleyn is dead, decapitated in the space of a heartbeat by a hired French executioner. As her remains are bundled into oblivion, Thomas Cromwell breakfasts with the victors. The blacksmith’s son from Putney emerges from the spring’s bloodbath to continue his climb to power and wealth, while his formidable master, Henry VIII, settles to short-lived happiness with his third queen before Jane dies giving birth to the male heir he most craves.\n\nCromwell is a man with only his wits to rely on; he has no great family to back him, no private army. Despite rebellion at home, traitors plotting abroad and the threat of invasion testing Henry’s regime to the breaking point, Cromwell’s robust imagination sees a new country in the mirror of the future. But can a nation, or a person, shed the past like a skin? Do the dead continually unbury themselves? What will you do, the Spanish ambassador asks Cromwell, when the king turns on you, as sooner or later he turns on everyone close to him?\n\nWith The Mirror &amp; the Light, Hilary Mantel brings to a triumphant close the trilogy she began with Wolf Hall and Bring Up the Bodies. She traces the final years of Thomas Cromwell, the boy from nowhere who climbs to the heights of power, offering a defining portrait of predator and prey, of a ferocious contest between present and past, between royal will and a common man’s vision: of a modern nation making itself through conflict, passion, and courage.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3676299009416307430\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9170761670486715176">Kate Atkinson was born in York and now lives in Edinburgh. Her first novel, <i>Behind the Scenes at the Museum</i>, won the Whitbread Book of the Year Award and she has been a critically acclaimed international bestselling author ever since.<br/><br/>She is the author of a collection of short stories, <i>Not the End of the World</i>, and of the critically acclaimed novels <i>Human Croquet</i>, <i>Emotionally Weird</i>, <i>Case Histories</i>,</span>
  <span id="freeText9170761670486715176" style="display:none">Kate Atkinson was born in York and now lives in Edinburgh. Her first novel, <i>Behind the Scenes at the Museum</i>, won the Whitbread Book of the Year Award and she has been a critically acclaimed international bestselling author ever since.<br /><br />She is the author of a collection of short stories, <i>Not the End of the World</i>, and of the critically acclaimed novels <i>Human Croquet</i>, <i>Emotionally Weird</i>, <i>Case Histories</i>, and <i>One Good Turn</i>.<br /><br /><i>Case Histories</i> introduced her readers to Jackson Brodie, former police inspector turned private investigator, and won the Saltire Book of the Year Award and the Prix Westminster. <br /><br />When Will There Be Good News? was voted Richard &amp; Judy Book Best Read of the Year. After Case Histories and One Good Turn, it was her third novel to feature the former private detective Jackson Brodie, who makes a welcome return in Started Early, Took My Dog. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9170761670486715176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
